<div id="review_652280708" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/652280708" />
    <a title="Nigel Bird" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4939292-nigel-bird"><img alt="Nigel Bird" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442532979p2/4939292.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/652280708?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nigel Bird" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nigel Bird" href="/user/show/4939292-nigel-bird">Nigel Bird</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer652280708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5864634235227034257">Brazzaville Beach is a tremendous novel.<br/>Right from the beginning it has the feel of something rather unusual and for me there was a definite double-take moment when I realised I’d found my place.<br/>It’s centred around 2 main aspects of Hope Clearwater’s life, her time with her husband in the UK and her time without in Africa.<br/>The drive of the plot centres around Hope’s work observing chimpanzees in the world’s leading scientific project on the subject of the animals. She’s cottoned on to the fact th</span>
  <span id="freeText5864634235227034257" style="display:none">Brazzaville Beach is a tremendous novel.<br />Right from the beginning it has the feel of something rather unusual and for me there was a definite double-take moment when I realised I’d found my place.<br />It’s centred around 2 main aspects of Hope Clearwater’s life, her time with her husband in the UK and her time without in Africa.<br />The drive of the plot centres around Hope’s work observing chimpanzees in the world’s leading scientific project on the subject of the animals. She’s cottoned on to the fact that strange things are happening within her community of chimps that have taken themselves away from the main group. The chimps from the north are sending patrols into the southern territories and this is the cause for a lot of interest. Unfortunately for her, the more she finds out, the more she realises that her discoveries are contrary to the theories of her eminent bosses and it seems that they’ll go to any length to suppress her findings.<br />Weaving in and out of this African scene is her background and her relationship with her very driven husband who is a gifted mathematician. He’s obsessed by seeing things in different ways and interprets things with numbers and visual patterns. It’s a background that helps to explain Hope’s current situation and thinking, while providing a hugely interesting story in itself.<br />There’s plenty of what I’ve come to expect from William Boyd in here: <br />It’s quite addictive, which is quite often the case for me when reading his books.<br />There’s the wonderful detail in the characters and settings and he’s a bit like Hope’s husband in the way he can present what is commonplace in new ways that make it a pleasure to get to know people and place.<br />There are the asides that show a tremendous knowledge in a vast range of areas (or at least they seem to) that are interesting in themselves, but are also very relevant and helpful as part of a gentle analysis.<br />There’s the African setting, clearly understood and alive with the exotic.<br />I loved it. I feel like I’ve had a good workout and a huge amount of entertainment.<br />The sad thing is, I was reading a signed, hard-back, 1990 first edition and it’s borrowed from a friend. I’d so like to keep it on my shelves and have considered a few ways of explaining its loss (the cat ate it and the like), but it never worked on my teachers and I don’t suppose my conscience could take it these days.<br />A super story that you should check out.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5864634235227034257" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer734534801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15428465853422249373">I've been meaning to read Boyd for awhile now and this one presented a tenuous link to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/730510686' rel='nofollow noopener'>King Leopold's Ghost</a>: the beach gets its name from one of Henry Morton Stanley's contemporaries (de Brazza) and its main narrative is set in a chimpanzee research preserve within the Congolese jungle. More of a segue than a link, and any similarities end there. <br/><br/>The story opens in Brazzaville where the main character, Hope Clearwater, is working as an <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethology' rel='nofollow noopener'>ethologist</a> studying primate behaviour. During her time obser</span>
  <span id="freeText15428465853422249373" style="display:none">I've been meaning to read Boyd for awhile now and this one presented a tenuous link to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/730510686" rel="nofollow noopener">King Leopold's Ghost</a>: the beach gets its name from one of Henry Morton Stanley's contemporaries (de Brazza) and its main narrative is set in a chimpanzee research preserve within the Congolese jungle. More of a segue than a link, and any similarities end there. <br /><br />The story opens in Brazzaville where the main character, Hope Clearwater, is working as an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethology" rel="nofollow noopener">ethologist</a> studying primate behaviour. During her time observing chimpanzees, she's increasingly alarmed by the growing brutality between them; their aggression goes against the decades of research out of the reserve and presents many complications. I’ll leave it there, but this storyline is absorbing, fascinating, and remarkably told. <br /><br />Her path to the Congo is revealed over the course of her reflections. There are three main narrative threads, which could be laid end to end, but are revealed to the reader in parallel. They’ve all combined to bring Hope to this isolated, beach jungle in the heart of a country torn with civil conflict. <br /><br />This was a rare read that consumed me. I checked my critical eye at the door, so I’m not going to force a review here. I think Boyd might be a new favourite.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15428465853422249373" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3203049499" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3203049499" />
    <a title="Susan Stuber" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22227336-susan-stuber"><img alt="Susan Stuber" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539449394p2/22227336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3203049499?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan Stuber" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan Stuber" href="/user/show/22227336-susan-stuber">Susan Stuber</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22227336-susan-stuber?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3203049499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14967052293253078595">I suppose that the message of this book is how ironic it is that humans avidly study chimpanzees, presumptiously thinking that they are less intelligent than themselves, only to find, on closer inspection, (see the mad scientists, the ridiculous wars, the wanton cruelty), that humans, while maybe more "intelligent," are by no means "better" creatures.<br/>This premise is interesting and could make a good book. But I find this one is lacking in empathy, either for the chimpanzees or for any of the oth</span>
  <span id="freeText14967052293253078595" style="display:none">I suppose that the message of this book is how ironic it is that humans avidly study chimpanzees, presumptiously thinking that they are less intelligent than themselves, only to find, on closer inspection, (see the mad scientists, the ridiculous wars, the wanton cruelty), that humans, while maybe more "intelligent," are by no means "better" creatures.<br />This premise is interesting and could make a good book. But I find this one is lacking in empathy, either for the chimpanzees or for any of the other characters involved. The genial mathematician, while he is originally seen by the author's narrator as beguiling, becomes quite quickly a clownish wimp with a manic/depressive problem. The primate scientist also appears to be at first beguiling, soon becomes a cantankerous ego maniac. The jungle (human) fighting is considerably more confusing that the jungle (primate) fighting. But we get it, there isn't much difference, really. And while the humans are distressed about the own wanton cruelty they observe among their own kind, they seem even more distressed to see it among the chimpanzees, which somehow they romantically considered to be more peace-loving. <br />In the middle of all this is the main protagonist, Hope. She has a sister named Faith. Their parents are boring, we learn at a birthday party. <br />To give the book some more depth (hope and faith may not be enough), Boyd throws in a lot of mathematical theory. Mainly about turbulence. Because maybe, if the formula is right, it can explain all the turbulence in the world?<br />To make it a bit more lusty, he throws in quite a few explicite descriptions of chimpanzee bacchanal sex. <br />Sound like a winner?<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14967052293253078595" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2621718116" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2621718116" />
    <a title="Jane Watson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51155116-jane-watson"><img alt="Jane Watson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534363620p2/51155116.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2621718116?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane Watson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane Watson" href="/user/show/51155116-jane-watson">Jane Watson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2621718116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12163995744497687116">Ah, another great book from William Boyd! This one was quite different from his others - all about ecology and chimpanzee studies, set in Africa and a side story in the UK. The main character, Hope Clearwater was well drawn and you could feel her frustrations and tenseness during the book, the reason for which is slowly revealed in a series of flashbacks. I thought the African part of the book was very well done and the story involving the chimpanzees was harsh and lifelike. Made me shiver a bit</span>
  <span id="freeText12163995744497687116" style="display:none">Ah, another great book from William Boyd! This one was quite different from his others - all about ecology and chimpanzee studies, set in Africa and a side story in the UK. The main character, Hope Clearwater was well drawn and you could feel her frustrations and tenseness during the book, the reason for which is slowly revealed in a series of flashbacks. I thought the African part of the book was very well done and the story involving the chimpanzees was harsh and lifelike. Made me shiver a bit and you realise that chimps, although outwardly looking cutish, are really wild animals and not necessarily to be trusted.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12163995744497687116" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Cwbnxvw5N+aX0Fi1SlfBLf/WR3o7TMQ/LWhTd0quZvJkIAgoKnnwxBa9aArKWPDIEwvHnoRm080HKz/zONXbKA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

In such a tense, charged atmosphere, Oates reveals that there must always be a sacrifice—of innocence, truth, trust, and, ultimately, of lives. Their father, whose mental state always has been slightly precarious, is committed to an asylum in the country. The children are sealed off with their grandfather in a crumbling country estate accompanied by their sturdy and well-loved servant, Kate, and the predatory tutor, Miss Gallagher. A love story, The Inner Circle is narrated by John Milk, a virginal young man who in 1940 accepts a job as an assistant to Dr. Alfred Kinsey, an extraordinarily charming professor of zoology at Indiana University who has just discovered his life’s true calling: sex. For in his later years Kinsey (who behind closed doors is a sexual enthusiast of the first order) ever more recklessly pushed the boundaries both personally and professionally. While Boyle doesn’t resist making the most of this delicious material, The Inner Circle is at heart a very moving and very loving look at sex, marriage, and jealousy that will have readers everywhere Matt Ruff was only twenty-two when Fool on the Hill was first published, but with his novel he gave us a story that won over readers of every persuasion. Not your usual first effort, Fool on the Hill is a full-blown epic of life and death, good and evil, magic and love.Think of the imaginative daring of Mark Helprin’s Winter’s Tale. The zany popism of Tom Robbins’s Another Roadside Attraction. The gnomish fantasies of J.R. Tolkien. Think of these and you begin to get some idea of one of the most remarkable first novels to come along in years.In the world of Fool on the Hill dogs and cats can talk, a subculture of sprites lives in the shadows and underfoot (if you’re the sensitive type, or drunk enough, you might see them cavorting across the lawn), and the Bohemians, a group of Harley- and horseback-riding students dedicated to all things unconventional, hold all-night revels for the glory of their cause.Then there is Stephen Titus George, the novel’s youthful hero, who somehow finds himself the main player in a story that began well over a century ago. George is a mild-mannered flier of kites, a sometimes writer of bestselling fiction, and would-be knight looking for a maiden. George will find his girl and the century-old story will provide the proverbial dragon whose slaying will sanctify their love.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8170.William_Boyd">About William  Boyd</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8170.William_Boyd"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1266355107p3/8170.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8170.William_Boyd">William  Boyd
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,363

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15170005604497647914">Note: <i>William^^Boyd</i><br/><br/>Of Scottish descent, Boyd was born in Accra, Ghana on 7th March, 1952 and spent much of his early life there and in Nigeria where his mother was a teacher and his father, a doctor. Boyd was in Nigeria during the Biafran War, the brutal secessionist conflict which ran from 1967 to 1970 and it had a profound effect on him.<br/><br/>At the age of nine years he attended Gordonstoun school, in</span>
  <span id="freeText15170005604497647914" style="display:none">Note: <i>William^^Boyd</i><br /><br />Of Scottish descent, Boyd was born in Accra, Ghana on 7th March, 1952 and spent much of his early life there and in Nigeria where his mother was a teacher and his father, a doctor. Boyd was in Nigeria during the Biafran War, the brutal secessionist conflict which ran from 1967 to 1970 and it had a profound effect on him.<br /><br />At the age of nine years he attended Gordonstoun school, in Moray, Scotland and then Nice University (Diploma of French Studies) and Glasgow University (MA Hons in English and Philosophy), where he edited the Glasgow University Guardian. He then moved to Jesus College, Oxford in 1975 and completed a PhD thesis on Shelley. For a brief period he worked at the New Statesman magazine as a TV critic, then he returned to Oxford as an English lecturer teaching the contemporary novel at St Hilda's College (1980-83). It was while he was here that his first novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77837._A_Good_Man_in_Africa" title=" A Good Man in Africa" rel="nofollow noopener"> A Good Man in Africa</a> (1981), was published.<br /><br />Boyd spent eight years in academia, during which time his first film, Good and Bad at Games, was made. When he was offered a college lecturership, which would mean spending more time teaching, he was forced to choose between teaching and writing.<br /><br />Boyd was selected in 1983 as one of the 20 'Best of Young British Novelists' in a promotion run by Granta magazine and the Book Marketing Council. He also became a Fellow of the Royal Society of Literature in the same year, and is also an Officier de l'Ordre des Arts et des Lettres. He has been presented with honorary doctorates in literature from the universities of St. Andrews, Stirling and Glasgow. He was appointed Commander of the Order of the British Empire in 2005.<br /><br />Boyd has been with his wife Susan since they met as students at Glasgow University and all his books are dedicated to her. His wife is editor-at-large of Harper's Bazaar magazine, and they currently spend about thirty to forty days a year in the US. He and his wife have a house in Chelsea, West London but spend most of the year at their chateau in Bergerac in south west France, where Boyd produces award-winning wines.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15170005604497647914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
